O, sons, thus I have completed this book, which is the last of the science of medicine, for through its completion and the end it has brought to itself, and through its explanations and declarations, it is evident to me that I have filled you with this treatise, which is part of my surgical operations.
For our region and in our time, surgical operation is almost entirely private, despite the great importance of its science. Its investigation has been neglected; and only some minor descriptions can be found in the books of the Ancients, which correct many errors and hesitations, but close off the understanding of it and extend its force and art.
And it is clear to me that I should vitalize that part of this treatise according to the way of explanation and declaration and abbreviation; and in order that the forms of the cautery tools and other instruments of the work be added through the declaration, and for their preparation, which cause is necessary.
More so, the reason why a good artisan doctor does not use his hand well in this our time is that the art of medicine is long and involved, and one must be trained before being able to practice anatomy, which Galen knew well, understanding the youth of the members and their forms, as well as their joints and separations; and having knowledge of the bones, nerves, and tendons, their number, and their diseases; and of the pulse veins and quiet ones, and the places of their propulsion.
And for this, Hippocrates is said to have said that by name is good, more in the small work, and most of all in the art of the hand.
More so, we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this work; for those who do not know what we have said about anatomy, will not be free from error due to which many people will die, as I have seen too many of them. For some are formed in this science and act without that other knowledge and experience.
For I have seen a foolish and inexperienced doctor who caused an apostema scrophula in the neck of a woman, and damaged some arteries so that great bleeding occurred, causing the woman to die among her greatest sufferings.
And there was another doctor who almost extracted a stone from a man who had been suffering for some time, and the stone was large, and it came to him, and he removed that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I have already called for the stone to be extracted, and I saw that due to the size of the stone and the position of the patient, he would likely die.
And there was another doctor who took payment from a duke of our land for surgery, and he came upon a black slave who had a fracture in the thigh; and because of the doctor's ignorance, he removed the fracture, over the wound, with pillows and splints of strong structure, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then the dislocation according to his desires, and then the rejoining, and then the leaving for a few days and commanding us to release the bandage; and we remained thus until the thigh and foot recovered, and then proceeded to our detriment.
And I was called to him, and prepared to dislodge the stone, and peace and an attempt to relieve his pains were achieved.
But however, corruption was confirmed in the member, for I could not arrest that, and did not cease to walk the corruption in the member while the malady persisted.
And there was another doctor who perforated an oncological abscess, and it became ulcerated over several days, during which the malignancy of the lesion grew significantly in the presence of its lord.
The cause is that the pain is purely due to melancholic humors, and does not affect the entire body, but rather affects a member that suffers from being attacked by all.
And for this reason, my children, it is necessary that you devise the operation into two divisions: one in which the operation is associated with health, and another in which the work is dangerous in its major dispositions.
And I have already existed in all parts of this book in which the operation is accompanied by error and fear; therefore, it is necessary that you keep this and leave that, so that the leaves do not find a way to speak and deceive you.
And therefore take your arms with diligence and protection of God, and the patient with facility and firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God seeking salvation and praiseworthy success, and leave behind the terrible illnesses difficult to cure, and stand firm in your arms against what fears you, so that sitting in your faith and in your name you are not forsaken: for more remains to your glory, and better elsewhere and later in the world and beyond in your sanctity.
Gout has already been dissected in some monastic texts: Do not treat ill diseases, lest bad doctors go unnamed.
And I have already dedicated this book according to three chapters.
Begin the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered of the foot, on the forms of the instruments and on the tools of the cautery, and on what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on dryness and perforation, and on cups, and on sores, and on the extraction of arrows and similar, all categorized and ordered by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on the cure of扭伤和类似病症；按仪器形状分类的章节。头部涉及脚部，以及仪器的形式。
I will describe the quality of the irons of that cautery and its effects, and in which complications cauterization is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the quality of the cautery iron and its effects is long and prolix, subtle by science, and secretly hidden; much has already been said about it by wise men, and they are in disagreement. It suffices me to give a small part of their sermons, fearing lengthiness.
And thus it is said that a cautery applied to any complexion universally works on all matters or bodily substances, except for two complexes, which are complexes of solid matter.
In the complex of solid and dry matter, dehydration occurs; for some said that a cautery is beneficial for this, while others said the opposite, that a cautery is not appropriate in illness which is due to quality and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inappropriate that an illness that is solid and dry be healed by what is cautery and dry.
And argue against those who say the opposite, that by cautery one is cured in all solid and dry illnesses that come in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body to its humors to the complex of fire, you find the body is parched.
And I say according to the speech of those who have experienced this, that they have already discovered it several times through experimentation.
But I do not claim that it comes to me as one who has already been experienced and exercised in the capital of cautery by recent exercise, and who knows the diversity of human complexions and the disposition of illnesses in their manifestations, and the causes of those, and the accidents related to them, and the space of time for those.
In other complications, there is no need for you to fear, and especially in the wet and inflammatory diseases, for all doctors are agreed on those, and do not differ in using a cautery in them.
And I say, children, that concerning the secret of curing by cautery with fire and its goodness, about the burning medical cautery, it is because the fire is simple, and its action reaches only the member being cauterized and causes no great harm to other members in contact.
But the action of the burning medical cautery reaches that part in the member which it touches, and greatly causes illness in the member that is diseased and difficult to heal.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does nothing but superfluous things. And we are already known for this through experience and long service to the study of the art concerning the true causes; and for this reason, I am excused for the length of my discourse. If it were not that it is not written in my liberty in this pause, and that you know a secret about the fire, and the quality of its operation on the bodies and the way by which you expel diseases, then this discourse would be philosophical and demonstrative, which would be acute for your minds.
And be wise, children, that they do not agree on the time at which it is convenient to make a cautery and pause in late spring.
And I say that a cautery is convenient at all times, because the damage caused by the accident of time is submerged in the youth of what is burned by the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong boils, which do not suffer delay because it is feared that those that come from evil intent are greater than small damage entering through time.
And this is not good in your feet, children, for they only consider the common people and the foolish doctors, because in that illness which is cured by a cautery it is not convenient all the time, and they pause that consequence.
And the cause is not thus because they care for this, for a cautery is not other than in a place of medicine which alters the composition and dries the humors which are the cause of the venous pains.
Yet, however, an excessive cautery over the medicine for the sake of its efficacy and virtue of its operation and greatness of its lord.
It is possible that the patient's illness may return at some point in time, according to the complexity of the illness, its location, the virtue of the individual, and what is prepared within the body through the accumulation of superfluous substances, or what is acquired by the body in terms of foods and similar causes; but if the illness in which a cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluous substances and humidity, like a dental cautery for pain and similar to that, it is possible that such pain will not return, more so as this is done in small amounts.
The vulgar saying is that the cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a saying of doctors, not what we understand by it, for I believe it is not a cure that helps either medicine or any other cause close to the cautery.
The cause is the opposite; and I understand that the cautery is the last remedy of medicine only when one has administered the methods of cure for some illnesses, and those medicines have not been effective, and near the end of the illness, one administers the cautery, which then helps. And at this point, the last remedy of medicine is the cautery, not according to the understanding which I understand the vulgar and most of the foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that a gold cautery is better than an iron one, and they did not say this only because of the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place for cautery does not burn, since it is not absolutely perfect; for I have already proved and examined that it does not do this in all bodies as others do. A gold cautery is good and better than iron, as they say; but when you make a cautery with fire of gold, it is not up to you to declare, because it ignites according to the quantity you want due to the hardness of the gold, and prepares to receive fire; if you adjust over the ignition, the gold melts by the fire, and some sheets are cast into the fire for the craftsman to catch from that.
And for this, when making a cautery with iron, you should touch it more lightly and more precisely to the correct position.
And I have already ordered this chapter on the cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the foot in the leg, so that I delay the patient what he wants from me.
The first chapter.
This cautery addresses the cold and humidity over the servel accident of which there are two causes, these being the cause of rheumatism and various pains in the head, from the forehead to the ears flowing, as well as many sleep disorders, toothache, and giddiness, and generally all illnesses that come through coldness, such as paralytic conditions, rheumatism, and apoplexy, and similar symptoms in those diseases.
The form of this cautery is as follows: Primarily, you command the patient to be evacuated with a saline medicine for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient's condition, their age, and their habits.

And place that cautery over the marked spot with a point, in such a way that it descends gradually, and you rotate it.
And lift your hand lightly and look at the spot, and if you see any part of the bone being uncovered by a small amount of oil, as much as a grain of pepper, then lift your hand. If not, return your hand to that cautery or another, if it is cooled, and wait until something is visible on the bone as I have said to you.
And take a little salt and dissolve it over the spot, and leave it there for three days.
Then place your hand over the inflamed groin area, leaving the other one on the inside where the fire scar has been destroyed. Then apply the ointment of tetra pharmacon to the area until it is healed.
And we have already said that when the wound is opened wide, it heals better, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the knee should be cauterized inside the bone, and that the cautery should be left until it has ignited some part of the bone.
Then scrape away what is charred from the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that the cautery should reach the marrow, pressing firmly on the bone until it cools, and this is the method of Alphécatti. And they believe that from that area, the vapors of the head expire.
And keep the wound open for a long time, and then treat it until it is healed and consolidated.

And the first type of cautery is safer and better, I advise you; use that one and do so for health.

Chapter XVI.
Of the cautery for the eyelid when the skin is converted to the inner parts and fistula occurs.
When the skin of the fistula is converted to the inner parts, one must puncture the fistula. The cautery for this can be performed in two ways: either by using a cautery or by applying burning medicine.
If the cautery is to be made with fire, command the patient to present himself before you that the skin may be cauterized as those which are cleansed, when they are prolonged and equalized; and if you puncture it, you should place it at its origin. Then strengthen the fistulas of the patient with a bandage so that they do not rupture until the skin is grown back.
Then when they are born and equalized, place the head of the patient on your knee, and hold over the eyelids of the patient with a sign, which should be in the shape of a leaf of hawthorn, and is the beginning of the mark for the skin.
Then place the head in a pillow of cloth or on a mucilaginous substance over the eye.
Then ignite the cautery of which this is the shape.
Then apply the cautery over the shape marked, little by little in small spots, until the surface of the wound is burned, which appears like a hawthorn leaf, entirely apparent and proper.
A sign of healing from your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye now contracted and the skin around it elevated after the puncture of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it there for three days, and then place it over the head between the eyebrows until the scar is healed. And after apply the ointment where it shall be healed.
And if it returns through the skin for some cause near their origin and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again over that area as you did first.
And if the skin is on the eyelid, then apply the cautery there, lifting it to its natural place and ensuring that the skin does not remain loose.
And the cautery being medicinally burning, you command the patient to extend and equalize the skin, then make a cloth letter in the shape of a mulberry leaf.
And then take a well-known and effective ointment and some vinegar. Mix these well, and prepare this mixture without rubbing it; then spread it over the cloth which is made in the shape of a mulberry leaf, and place that over one or both wounds; and leave the cloth slightly loose, with the patient's head at the top.
And place it on the medicine and press it a little but do not wet it inside so that the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like that of fire.
And so, to perceive the bite of the cauterization, when you leave the medicine and wet that as you have said; then when the bite ceases, place the medicine again and wash it well with water; and then consider if the eyelid has already been raised, as it is washed, close it again with fire or incision, for then it is good; and if not, return to the place of the medicine where you did not press the medicine, and do not deface your operation until it is equalized.
And then place it on the swelling between the time that the bond of adherence is eradicated, and then treat it with a thick ointment or another method until it is healed.

Chapter 25.
From the nostrils at the ends of the nose.
Nodules appear in the nostrils at the ends of the nose, and these are enlarged and smoothed over in a few days as the swelling diminishes.
And for this, you should enter that which is at the beginning of the appearance of the lesion, and eradicate all of it. And place yourself over the area or the cautery or burning medicine where it was located.
But if the inflammation is delayed before it enlarges, consider whether it is hardened, dark in color, and has little sensation. In that case, do not touch that with a sharp instrument as it is an abscess; for nodules often accompany this abscess due to its great malice towards the area where it was located.
And if the abscess is due to a blow and not dark in color, and the inflammation can occur throughout that area, then drain the poison and cut there without fear, and treat the area with what dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
For the suturing of the nose and lips when continuity is dissolved by ulcer or by similar conditions.
Know that when there is dissolution of continuity in one of these cartilages, you should not rely much on that operation but rather on several skilled workers.
Then probe, when you have to deal with any of these causes, whether it is a fresh ulcer and its discharge, and then adjust the two edges of the ulcer with sutures, and proceed to cure that until it is healed.
And if the edges of the dissolution of continuity are already separated, and any one is made healthy, then probe that you should remove each part of its underlying tissue until it discharges pus. Then adjust the two edges with sutures and strengthen them, and sprinkle dragon's blood powder and triple distilled vinegar over them, and place a piece of diaphonic plaster or another consolidated plaster on top of the powder, and leave this paste for two to three days. Then remove it and change the plaster, leaving it until the roots are absorbed, and proceed to cure with ointment until it is healed.
The manner of suturing is that you add the solution of continuity or use needles as we have recommended in the belly suture, or add the solution of continuity with thread as you are made to know.
Chapter XXVII.
Of the extraction of those causes that venous problems occur in the two lips within the internal parts.
Patches appear in the internal parts of their lips, turning into hard abscesses resembling lard. Some are small, while others are larger.
Therefore, carefully incise all parts of the hard swellings in the lips and cavities, then fill the wound with three layers of cloth until the wound is tightened, and subsequently wash it with vinegar and treat the area with water that has astringent properties until it heals.
Chapter XXVIII.
Of the incision of flesh adhered to the gums.
Great care must be taken over the gum attached to the flesh, which the ancients called albulus. Then extract that which you hold with one instrument and divide it carefully, and let the healthy blood flow or the venom.
Then place something warm on the area for about three minutes or another styptic or excitatory powder.
And if that flesh returns, then divide it again, and cauterize it; for after the cauterization, it will not return.
Capitol.xxix.
On the reason for teeth hurt.
When on the surface of the teeth that are split inside and outside, and between the gums, there appear sharp, hard edges, some times they are blackish and are caused by yellow or green deposits between the gums due to corruption, which cause the teeth to become loose.
And therefore make sure that you cut the patient's thigh between your thighs and place his head in the crook of your arm, and rub the teeth where these edges and similar causes appear with sand so that they do not break for any reason, and do this from blackness and yellowishness and other changes in them.
So if what remains of the matter at the beginning of the reasoning is clear, that is good; and if not, then you iterate the reasoning to those matters the next day and the second and the third until it comes to the final statement in what you want.
And know that teeth have needed to be extracted in various forms and with different figures according to how your operation is prepared. And so, for that reason, the extractor used when the tooth is extracted from the inside is different from the extractor used when it is between the teeth according to other forms. These forms of extractors you should prepare, which you will often see.
Capitol.xxx.
For the extraction of teeth.
Cure the tooth with great skill and take your time in the entire extraction process, for no cause can compensate for its absence when extracted, for it is a substance that sublimes during the removal.
When the patient is forced to remove a tooth, you must be certain which tooth is painful, checking several times where the pain originates and ensuring it is not in a healthy tooth. Once you extract the diseased and painful tooth, the pain generally ceases, for we have seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, you must be certain which tooth is painful, and knowing this well, when you incise along the circuit of the tooth in the socket where any virtue lies, ensuring that it is dissolved from the entire part of the tooth and gum, then insert those tweezers or fine scissors gradually at the beginning, ensuring that the tissue dissolves.
And then irrigate it well to strengthen the grip for good speech or strong fracture, and place the head of the patient between your hands so that you hold them securely without causing discomfort, and then extract the tooth in a straight manner so as not to break it, nor give the patient additional pain.
And if it is not forced out, then take one of those instruments, and place it under the tooth with great ease and effort, ensuring its removal is done similarly to how it was initially placed.
If the tooth is broken or corroded, then you should fill that hole with cloth and tie it at the end of a fine thread to prevent it from breaking during your operation on that area.
And you should operate exquisite[ly] with incision in the circuit of all parts of the gum and guard maintaining your strength so that you do not break that part of it, and return over the gout through its malice which is worse than the first pain of it.
And guard yourself that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their incisions and audacity regarding the eradication of gout; for they do not administer what we have advised. For spots and marks often bring great diseases upon men which are harder to break than broken bones, and the reasons for their failures all or some of them, or that you extract that part from certain bones of the mandible thus for spots and marks we have testified.
And for the eradication of gout, wash the mouth with wine or vinegar mixed with salt.
And if bleeding occurs for this reason, as when small blisters appear, then take a little quantity of vinegar and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that vinegar does not stop it, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and delicate tweezers used to extract a tooth at the beginning should be long and straight, short and thick, so that they do not bend when those you need to use.
And they should be such that when you tighten them around those, they do not bend, and are long but thin.
And they should be made of Indian steel, straight and long. At the ends of these, there are teeth such that one enters the other and tighten by descending construction firmly. Sometimes the ends of these are in the shape of a heron's bill, which is called a ciconia, and they are strongly constructed.

Capitol.xxxii.
Of the cure of the prominent teeth over others.
When the teeth are prominent not according to their natural form, especially when they protrude in women, and particularly in venerable old women; therefore consider this: if a tooth is close to another tooth and it is impossible to extract or treat it with a needle, then remove that tooth; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then cut out that tooth using this instrument, which resembles the head of a pig, and made of Indian steel with an extremely sharp point.
And let your incision be where you cut that in too many days due to the hardness of the tooth, and may it not be another of the teeth.
And if the tooth is prominently protruding and needs to be filed, then file that with a file of Indian iron, which is of this form:
And let it be entirely of Indian iron; and let the margin be filed finely; and let it have small pointed teeth like a file, with which the tooth can be filed little by little over many days for ease, so that no other tooth may decay or fall out. Then lengthen it at the tip, and sharpen it with a few razors.
And if it is a tooth of which one part is fractured and you file the tongue, then cut that such that the sharpness of that fracture is obstructed and equalized, and may it be filled so that the tongue does not hurt nor the word become corrupted.
Book XXXIII.
For the restoration of decayed teeth using gold or silver filler.
When accidents occur to the front teeth due to concussion or removal by impact or during hunting, and the patient cannot strengthen what is left, and after treating it with astringent medicines your treatment has not been effective, then for those that are strong fill them with gold and not silver, because silver turns color and will likely leak in a few days, but gold does not change over time.
And the filling should be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the form of the cavity is that you take the gold and place twice its amount between two healthy teeth, and soon it will appear with two twists of the gold between two decayed teeth, or if one or several, until the tissue reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then insert the tissue into the part where you started, and strengthen your hand with ease and skill until it is not decayed.
And let your bandage be such that it holds the tooth roots firmly in place so that they do not slip out.
And then divide the excess roots of the tooth with forceps or sharp scissors, and add them to any location with your hand, and place both in between the healthy tooth and the decayed one, so as not to injure the tongue. And let it be thus tied like a Roman.
If it is slipped or broken, hold it with another thread in such a manner throughout its length.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the extraction of two healthy teeth and two decayed ones, as you see these tied:
And when one returns a tooth or two to their proper place in their sockets, and they are grown back as we have described, and remain embedded.
And this is done by skilled artisans.
And sometimes it is suitable for certain causes of the bones of cattle, and it is made from them like a form of tooth, and is placed in the location where the tooth failed, and is strengthened as we have said, and remains and serves you long.
Chapter XXXIV.
Of the incision of the ligament which we call and prohibit speech.
This ligament, which we call and sometimes leaves the tongue in some cases naturally as one is born, or accidentally due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation is that one opens the patient's mouth, which contains the tongue; then raises the tongue, and then incises that ligament nerve according to the width between which the tongue can be freed from its attachment.
But if there is any hardness or induration in that area, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you cut one time and incise that ligament nerve according to the width at which you have already incised the ligament and dissolved the induration.
And make sure that no crack is made in the subcutaneous layer, and carefully split here to avoid a flow of blood.
And then wash the incision edges with warm water or apply a wet cloth; and place the tongue depressor over them; if needed, let it stay during each night to prevent the wound from becoming consolidated again.
But if there is a flow of blood, place a glass vessel over the area; but if the bleeding continues, then cauterize the location with a pointed cautery suitable for this purpose. And then apply other treatments as necessary until it is healed.
Capitol.xxxv.
For the extraction of a ranula, use the tongue depressor.
Sometimes, an abscess appears, causing the tongue to swell like a small lump, prohibiting its natural operation; and as it fills the mouth, the swelling becomes more significant.
And the operation for this is to make an incision in the groin sixty centimeters in front of the pubis and consider the abscess.
And if you see one of reddish-brown or black, hard, to which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that area.
And if it has turned white, which indicates humidity, then you place the probe in it, and incise that area with a thin blade, and incise all parts; and if you notice blood during your operation, then pause above zegi trissat, which is vitriol, within which you restrain the blood.
And then return to your work after having completed it, after it has been washed with wine and salt; and care for that with the other treatment until it is healed.
Capitol.xxxvi.
On the extraction of abscesses from both ears and those that occur in the throat, as well as other abscesses.
When in the throat gout, so are their glands those which are gathered cotton-like, which occur externally and are named two ears, which when you cure them as we said in the division and it is not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, of dusky color, of little sensation, then do not touch that with iron.

And if it is of white color, radish-like, and the root of it is fine, then that is to be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has fully matured and rested after cautery, or if it has diminished with some reduction; then make an incision in the morning before the sun, place its head in your lap, and open its mouth. The servant taking between his hands the patient's tongue, holds it with the most noble instrument of which this is the form:

If there is a flow of blood, guard with water from decoction of herb of myrrh or leaf of holly and similar to that of the causes styptical until the flow of the blood is restrained, and then cure that among which it is healed.
Sometimes this occurs in the throat, other abscesses elsewhere on the two ears, and incise those according to my judgment and as described in the incision of two mallets equally.
And I had already treated a woman with an abscess which was located in her throat, which abscess had a dark color, little sensation, and had already closed her throat. The woman breathed very thinly, and had already been forbidden to eat and drink water, and she was nearing death if more water remained for one or two days.
And the abscess had already been pressing so long that it had been two weeks before it would heal through two incisions.
Then I incised it forcefully and clamped one of her ounces, drew out that portion, and then incised the other, and closed its opening, held her tongue, and placed the ounce in the abscess.
And then you cut away a large part of it, and there was but little blood flowing from it. Therefore, the swelling was that woman's gout, and she began to drink water soon, and then consumed some food. Then I could not cease incising that abscess many times for a long time, and an abscess would occur in its place, which continued to swell as long as that lasted.
Therefore, I treated the gout on her and applied cautery; then I walked about and did not know what God had done to that one near me.
Capitol.xxxvii.
On incising the swelling when it is abscessed.
When catarrh affects the uvula, and it is abscessed and long, then it is named perpendicular; if it is in the part with a large and round swelling, then it is called a bubo.
When it is cured as we have said in the division, and you do not see your cure, then often you find that the abscess has already come to an end and become subtle. Then you should incise the one that is aggregated and round; if it is of a length, and is bloody, with a dusky or black color, and is not more than a centimeter, then you must fear the incision of it, for there is fear of gout in that.
And when you see that according to the manner that I have said of its whiteness and length, you make an incision in the patient and probe the wound with an instrument of which it will take the shape and then place the onion inside the new opening, and pull that down and cut that with one of the two instruments which are called in the cutting of two bellies.
And make sure that you do not cut there but where it is attached to the natural term of addition, for if you cut beyond it, you will damage its function.
And near the incision, use what we have recommended in the cutting of two bellies, and cure that until it is healed.
And if the patient was frightened by the incision, make sure you use something to calm him during the cautery. The way of applying the cautery is simply with hot water. You place the head of the patient in your hand, and then probe the wound with the instrument we have mentioned. Then take from the water which we have described in the chapter on cautery, and apply it moderately but not too thin to the area, filling the cavity of the instrument whose shape this is:
Let the lower part of the cavity in which the medicine is placed be as wide as the width of a ray fish, and place the instrument on top of the medicine near the vulva, with the patient's buttocks below to catch any saliva or mucus from the mouth of the patient that might enter through the internal medicine, so that it does not get contaminated by this cause and spoils.
Place your hand on the medicine, and express it over the uvula for half an hour, until you see that it is darkened and saturated by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take some cloth and wrap it around the end of the ray, submerge the cloth in the medicine, and insert the ray through the cloth into a tube so that the cloth adheres within the tube. Do this several times until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the uvula, then let it dry and fall off in about three or four days.
And if you need to apply more medicine, apply it, and after cauterizing, clean the circuit of the uvula with a cloth soaked in vinegar, modify what is in the circuit of the uvula due to the medicine, and wash it with fresh water, treat externally with ointment, and internally with decoctions until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and shorter in duration of fear.
Some cases of uvula pain are cured with what is finer than cautery and incision, which is treated by fumigation in this manner: take calamus, hyssop, origanum, rue, scikos arabicus, camomile, grind them into a paste similar to that of herbs, and adjust all these causes or some of them into one vessel, immerse them in water well boiled; let the vessel be covered properly, with an opening in the middle on which place an iron container according to this manner; make it of silver or iron.
And place the extremity in the mouth of the patient where the vapor rises to the uvula above the gullet, so that the uvula is well saturated.
And then repeatedly apply it to those affected areas where it is dry, and guard that you do not perform this treatment at the beginning of the onset of carbuncles, as it would too tightly constrict the carbuncle. You should not do this, but instead declare the presence of a carbuncle with a cautery.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a cane and place a cork at the end of it, so that the mouth of the patient is opened, because the cork prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good for health.
Capitol. XXXVIII.
Of the extraction of thorns and what adheres in the wound and other causes.
Spots or thorns that adhere in the wound or from a foreign object.
Then carefully extract what is obvious and visible. Press the tongue with an instrument in front of the light so that you may clearly see what is in the wound.
And what is not apparent and hidden in the wound, make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because if a strong cause attached to the wound causes vomiting, it will be expelled; or have the patient swallow a piece of napkin or rue cloth or a chunk of dry bread; or take a piece of sponge well soaked in wine, tie it tightly with thread, and make the patient swallow this, for it will reach the place where the thorns are located and be expelled. And take an eel and bind it with honey, place it inside and secretly extract it; or take a probe and secretly insert it and then remove it.
And if none of these causes we have mentioned apply, then use a lead instrument in this form:
And let it be as thick as a small finger, and at the end of it, have a cavity that you can insert into his throat with ease.
And make his head lift up, and keep watch that the touch of his bone in the throat reaches his body, or that the doctor places his hand there.
And instead, when the patient himself inserts honey, by his knowledge of the place of the cause and pushes it down, and pulls his hand towards the instrument, do all this according to the quantity prepared inside before it is completely inserted.
Capitol.xxxix.
From the extraction of gouty fluid associated with the gauloar.
When gouty fluid is treated with the medicines as we have said in the division of the cure with medicines and you do not agree, consider at the gauloar of the malate under the sun, for you have understood the language of the patient with the instrument which we have recommended.
So if you see the gouty fluid, extract it either through a small needle or with fine, delicate instruments.
And if it is not possible to extract it there, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the gauloar of the malate, and place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, performing this as many times as necessary (until the gouty fluid is dead and comes out of the gauloar), and let the patient abstain from all water for the entire day.
And then take a sponge full of water, open its mouth over the area, and wash the area with it; and do not swallow any part of it, but let the water remain on the area for about an hour with your hand, as soon as the gouty fluid perceives the water it will start to come out.
If not fogged properly as we have advised, then subfog the thigh with cyme or assafoetida through the instrument we have advised in the uvula fogyation.
Do this, so that it may fall into place.
And another way of performing the fogyation is that you take a brazier in which are burning coals and cover the brazier with a cloth in the middle of which there is a perforated tube, and place the end of the instrument at the other end of the tube; then proceed with the subfog; and place the thigh over the end of the canula, and seal its mouth so that the subfog does not escape until you see that the subfog has reached the uvula, for the uvula will soon fall.
And if not cured, then repeat the subfog several times, and make the patient abstain from drinking, eating salty foods and such, and not to drink water. Thus it is necessary that this regimen be followed.
And this is the form of the instrument used to treat gout when it is seen; and it resembles tongs as you see them, except that there is some curvature in it. And it is placed on the gout, and the extremity of it is similar to the beak of a stork, which is curved, and is filed with a file, so that when any cause is compressed, it does not leak.
Capitol.xl.
In which the main discourse on boils and their treatment is presented.
Boils are special kinds of lumps, occurring in various ways, as remembered, for each one in this treatise.
And they vary in penetration and treatment by two methods. Of these, one is specific to the nature of boils themselves; and it concerns the compression of the humors.
And the second method is specific to the places where bodily accidents occur. A boil that occurs on the head is different from one that occurs in the genitals; and a boil in fleshly tissue is different from one at a joint. For each of them, there is a distinct procedure for operation.
And more of abscesses some of which cover that they not be perforated but near maturation of the health in those or complement of their.
And other abscesses some of which cover that they be drawn out when they are ripe, and not matured according to their complement, thus as abscesses that are close to joints, for an abscess that is near a joint, the nature of it is prolonged into where you will be here that is in the circuit of it, and greatly corrupts the ligaments or the nerves of that joint, so that it becomes a cause of contraction of that limb; or it is an abscess close to the main member, and if you delay the perforation of it until its maturity affects the main member; or it is near the anus, therefore pierce that which is not ripe; and it must not be pierced raw, due to poor digestion, but one that is near the anus, so that you do not cause a prolapse and be extended into the internal parts of the anus, and a fistula be formed, or something done in its term that does not heal. And you must know the time for perforating the abscess, and this is when it is ripe.
And know that it is ripe when there is the cessation of pain and the end of fever, and nearly the decrease of redness and pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And know that when you can perceive the penetration of pus in the location below the abscess and coming from the healing of the prominence, then proceed.
And the penetration of pus should be made along the length of the flesh or the body, if the abscesses are in the thigh or the feet, and at the location of the lacertae, the cords of the nerves, the arches; and, finally, in all locations where there is no reflection; for in those that do reflect, use the penetration according to that member.
When the abscess is in a fleshy location, it is better to delay the penetration until the digestion of the pus is confirmed according to our previous saying; for if you penetrate it prematurely, it will prolong the course of the disease and make the edges and depth more pronounced.
Sometimes abscesses are pierced according to the width of the flesh or body as necessity demands, or according to that from which the member suffers.
And ensure that for small abscesses, you perform a small puncture; in large abscesses, make a large and wide puncture or multiple sections according to the size of the abscesses.
And in other cases of abscesses, ensure that the cavity is closed and sealed. When this is done, do it while the pus has stopped flowing, just as you would when dealing with large boils and buboes.
And of those that are incised with a section having three angles; and in which an incision is made as the shape of a mulberry leaf, just as with abscesses of ganglions.
And of those in which a circular section and a crescent section are administered, resembling those.
Otherwise, for those abscesses that do not require it as with superficial abscesses, ensure that you perform a superficial puncture or a flat one only.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected pure matter, that you perforate it and do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted one part, then on another day inject the abscess with a larger part, and continue to do so in stages until it is thoroughly treated.
And especially if the patient is of a choleric temperament, or a woman in labor, or a small child, or an old person in decrepitude, for the animal spirit is resolved with the emergence of the pure matter, and greatly alleviates the pain, so you should openly relieve it.
Take great care with this chapter, and when you make your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, ensure that you cleanse the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or a flat section, then administer either hot or cold remedies accordingly; if the abscess is large, and the cracks of the perforation are painful, then you should place your hand in each tract until they heal together.
And if in the abscess a part of the cord is divided or you have concaved it in the circuit, then you should close it with cold flax or a thread made of linen cloth, as when you have removed bandages or roots, their humidity; strengthen it on the third day; and proceed with it and cure it according to what is appropriate from ointments until it is healed.
If blood flows during the time of your operation, then administer water mixed with wine in which you soak a cloth, and place that over the area of the bleeding several times.
If the bleeding persists, then you should administer treatment and cure with powders which I have recommended in many places of this our book and division.
If it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is near major nerves, then you should infuse powders in wine and oil, and place those over the areas.
And if it is in the summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should place powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all this should be infused within the first three days, as we have said, and then you should incise the abscess, clean it, and administer to it what is appropriate for its cure until it heals.
And this is what is necessary concerning the knowledge of the cure of abscesses universally, but according to the division I have already said about all abscesses in what manner the cure thereof should be treated individually.
Chapter XL.
Of abscesses where the vein is in the chain of the head.
In the chain of the head, small abscesses, and their species of knots which contain tunics that are formed; and the species of these are spots.
And of these they are lumps, and of those that contain humidity resembling lime. And of those that contain humidity resembling dust, and similar to them; and of those that are hard, like stones.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, just as you should not cause resistance in the extraction of those.
And the operation in the section of these is that you express them with an instrument of the form which is nearby, which is called a probe until you know it contains.
If what it contains is humidity, then section that along its length by superficial section according to this form:
And begin at the section of B line between the line of G. Once the humidity is evacuated, then scrape that skin which contains that humidity, and leave no cause of all, for too many times returns if there remains any cause of all.
And then immerse it in Egyptian ointment if present, otherwise in water and salt; then apply it to the wound and leave it there for another day, for in this way you will dissolve all that is beneath the skin.
And then repeatedly apply the ointment to that part with Egyptian ointment, gently if you feel it necessary, until you are confident that there is no underlying cause of that skin issue; then treat the wound with ointment once it is healed.

In this hernia, nothing has been seen that has been burned during the treatment of it.
And the first will say that it is done this way as we have recounted in the hernia case; which is that you tie the vessels near the section at that facilitation of it; and dry out in the middle location around the apostema with what I operate there, before the vessels fall.
And cure the plague as we have said until it is healed.
Capitol LXVII.
Of the disorder that occurs in the ankles.
In some cases, when there is a rupture in the ankles as we have said, apply a poultice to the area.
And the geniuses do not attribute any cause of the intestines to the geniuses; and if they do, it is rare; and return them to their place at all times.
But if the time at which this occurs is prolonged, there is an adjusted fissure in the cavity into which one places the intestine; and this happens due to the extension of the cavity, which is in the ankles as we have said. The cause is that the cavity is stretched, and then softened, and the area becomes an apostema and poultices are applied.
And the treatment of it is with a cautery as it is preceded by the narration of it.
Sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and the patient lies on his side between your hands, and then the place of the rupture of the abscesses dries up by exsanguination according to the breadth as much as three fingers.
And then strengthen the white cloth that is under the cuff when it is uncovered.
And then take the wick and place it over the prominent part of the cuff, and press it against the root of the belly.
And then sew the two prominent parts over the end of the wick of the cuff, and adjust one to the other with the stitching.
And then extract the end of the wick, and do not go outside the cuff entirely, nor extend the cloth, nor any other cause as I have faith in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then cure that in the treatment of ulcers, and as you incise the flaps, clean it and restore the ulcer before it is consolidated; for the cuff contracts and does not adjust the prominence of the rupture.
And cautery is better in this place, for it is more closely related to health.
Capitol. lxviii.
Of the mollification of the skin of the testicles.
When the skin of the testicles is spotted in some men, it becomes softened because the flesh that lies between them is softened, and attention is paid to this.
Then it is necessary for the one who is responsible for the treatment to ensure that the patient remains in a position conducive to healing according to their condition, and entirely over the area that has become softened, placing it above the natural skin; and then he should adjust two flaps at the suturing site.
And if you wish, begin the closure of the softened excess skin with three or four stitches and a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and afterwards, treat for the recovery of other wounds until they are healed and the stitches fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Of castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but for cause I am told to remember it in this free treatise; and the recollection of it is necessary for two ways, of which one is so that when asked about it by a physician, he knows the cure for him upon whom it happens; and the other way is that we may have need of castration for our young animals for those like mules, and bulls, and pigs, and similar to animals.
And thus it is said that castration can be performed in two ways: either by atrissio or by fissure by section.
But in that which is performed by atrissio, the way of the operation is that we make the beast stand in cold water until the genitals of it become softened and relaxed. And then take those with your hand until they become relaxed and no marks appear upon the touch.
In castration caused by rupture and drying, make sure you have the animal and press the cord of the testicles with your left hand.
Then lift the suspensory ligament, and incise over each groove between two processes.
Then incise those pus that have exuded in each case. And do not incise any of the veins in the ciphac, but incise only the thin vein which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better which is done by traction, for traction firmly leaves no cause of the testicles so that the animal desires copulation.
Then cure the wound while it is healing.
Capitol. lxx.
Of the cure for hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in males occurs according to two methods: one where there is a gap in the cord of the testicles, in which it appears between the two testicles and partly female in nature, with its skin.
And sometimes a flow of urine comes from there which is in the channel of the testicles.
In women, it is special: and the woman's mons pubis has two folds like testicles, a little softer to the touch like a swelling in the groin similar to those of a man with his testicles.
And the cure is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. And you should probe where the flesh is tight so as to relieve the pain there, and then apply the cure for other wounds.
And in the second kind for men from which urine comes from the channel of the testicles, neither cure nor operation is needed.
Capitol.xxI.
On the labia and flesh protruding due to causes in women.
Labia firmly attached is close to natural causes where it is strained and appears legible upon examination; sometimes in some women it is magnified so much that it stretches like in men, and may protrude during intercourse.
Then you should have the superfluous mucus of the throat in your hand or spoon and ingest it.
And do not finish with ingestion, especially in the region of the rectum, so that no flux of sperm occurs.
And then care for those wounds until they are healed.
More concealed flesh is flesh that arises in the horror of the uterus until it fills that space, and a hard lump is situated outside according to the likeness of a clot (and for this some first called it "caudate illness"); therefore, you should cut it thus as we cut mucus, and care for it until it is healed.
Capitol.lix.
On the care of alratica.
Alratica is when the hymen of the woman is not perforated, or is slightly torn. And it can be natural or accidental.
Accidental pain arises from an illness that has preceded, and this is caused either by unclothed skin or by a thin or thick cloak.
It is felt in the groin of the thigh, or in the sides of it, or in the upper part of it, or in the lower part of it, and you prohibit intercourse and pregnancy, and strongly you prohibit menstruation.
The knowledge of this is made by looking at the midwife, if the obstruction is obvious, probe gently; but if it is not obvious, then you search for that among the thighs or on the abdomen.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin object, and is close to the two lips, then you open and separate that.
And you place powders on the two lips, and then place your fingers indicating both sides near them.
And the woman is on her back, her thighs extended, and then spread the two lips with force until that thin object is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool and soak it in oil, place it over the area, and keep someone there to prevent the incised area from healing too quickly.
If the swelling is large and thick, then incise that area with a lancet or a leaf of mint.
If the swelling is due to flesh, then suspend that area with a pin, and incise that area.
And use those medicines that stop the flow of venom from a bite, such as garlic, dragon's blood, and haws, mixed with honey.
And then administer a broad lead tube to prevent the ulcer from healing too lightly.
And keep it for a few days and administer boiled water.
And then use the other healing method for consolidation once it is healed.
And sometimes accidents occur in the natural abundance of other tissues, then you must incise according to this method. If this is due to an abscessed boil, in the major abscess do not create any counter-irritation by applying a iron in any manner.
Chapter LXXIII.
For the treatment of hemorrhoids and prolapse and of red boil that occurs in the vagina of women.
Hemorrhoids are appreciated as the result of small veins situated between which constantly flow dark blood; and as for old hemorrhoids, they form knots.

Therefore, say that hemorrhoids and knots when in the prolapse of the rectum and do not appear in the sensation, then in those do not make any treatment with a iron.
But those that are in the rectal orifice and on which there is sensation, these are the ones that are treated.
And ensure that you insert the female part in warm water and then extend the thighs with the scissors or a rough cloth, and incise those areas with razors.
And then sprinkle over those areas the flux of the blood from one of the incisive powders seven times with bite marks, thus causing a similar effect to those.
And then wrap his feet for an hour, and then immerse that in cold water.
And if the bleeding persists, then apply that salve in a decoction of sumac and of slices of garlic and of walnuts and of similar substances.
And then take flour of oats and mix it with honey and wine, and make an ointment with this over his back.
And if the bleeding is stopped, that is good; otherwise, apply it to two flaxseeds, and make seven incisions on the breasts.
And then take wool and immerse it in the juice of caraway or in the juice of angelica or in the juice of garlic in fermented wine, and apply it to the area; and cure the area with ointments until it is healed.
And cure that which is within and let it be healthy.

Chapter 75.
To teach midwives how to properly treat breech births when they occur according to an unnatural position.
Necessary cause is that the midwife recognize at the beginning the natural figure of childbirth by signs. When you see the female expel from the passages and desire to smell the air, and is moistened by what is over her during labor, then you know that this birth is according to a natural figure, and the expulsion is over her head, and the second one is either suspended or hanging from the navel.
When you see those signs, then compress his belly so that the fetus descends lightly; when it presses on its own head, let it bring the second one, and it is cleansed of those superfluities by good purification.
That which is another to this part is unnatural and unacceptable, for when the fetus rests on its feet and the body of the mother is resting in front of the head of the fetus and its feet, or one of its hands, or at its side, or the head of the fetus and one of its hands, or over its belly, and firmly leans on its own back, resembling figures that are unacceptable.
For necessary cause the midwife must be wise and subtle in all those causes, and she must guard against error and deception.

For the treatment of fistulas that discharge pus.
The fistulas that discharge pus below are often due to swelling and enlargement of those located near the buttocks or in the space of one side.
And it is either a fistula or abscess.
And when that swelling is old and caused by watery white moisture or subtle rot, sometimes these fistulas penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by what is caused by them through the discharge and windy condition; sometimes the patient uses the opening, and a strong exhalation occurs from those worms.
And of these fistulas, sometimes those that penetrate enter into the bladder and into the flesh of the vagina; and of those that penetrate into the joint of the thigh and into the notch of the buttock.
And to know whether a penetrating fistula enters the buttock or does not, you should insert your finger into the buttock and gently probe with a thin wire or needle in the fistula when there is no pus.
But if there is turbidity, then insert a thin tube of lead or horsehair between which you feel the tube or hair in your hand.
And if you do not feel that at all nor proceed from it any cause of evacuation or worm or flatulence, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrant, and then apply something to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrant into the bladder or into the flesh of the penis, then its significance is dysuria from that and prohibition thereof until the area with medicine is consolidated.
But if it is penetrant into the junction of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the circuit, then its sign is that the tube passes through here if there is no turbidity in the bone, and pain in the circuit of the location; and that pus constantly flows from it; and that it does not respond to that treatment, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, complete cure is not achieved, and the cure of those is work and waste of the surgeons' leaves upon which they firm themselves.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, heal with iron as I will recount to you later, and what I have already said stands.
And it is that the patient lies on his back with his legs elevated between his thighs, the hips inclined towards the belly of the patient, and then insert a lead or iron tube, if it is in the tortuous fistula, into the place where you can feel the pulse.
And if the patient feels pain around the throat, then you must insert your thumb along the throat.
And if you feel the pulse already clearly and spontaneously passing through your thumb, or if you feel something between your thumb and the gout or flesh, then you can certainly determine that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for healing does not occur there, as we have said.
But some say that it is cured in some men after a while.
And concerning the treatment in which healing is hoped for, you must ignite a fine cautery according to the size of the fistula, as is previously described, and insert that burning instrument into the fistula until it reaches the throat; and then repeatedly do this two or three times until you can feel that all those grafted tissues around the fistula are firmly attached, resembling the quill of a bird.
And then cure that which is with joints wrapped in bandages between where all those muscles are contracted, and then cure that which is with medicines the flesh generating and consolidating, and if it is healed, well; and if not, then it is not healed in some time by another cure.

Of the treatment of the wounds.

From the extraction of arrows.

And of the signs of the arrow is when it lodges in the brain, passes through the bone, and is situated above the ventricle which is over the brain, for it happens by strong saline discharge, scotoma, vertigo, redness in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of understanding, and vomiting of bile, and greatly the speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed, and situated at the place of the wound a white moisture resembling dust, and from it resembling the humidity of flesh.

If the fracture is on the outer parts, then you should place another piece of the bone above the stone and another piece from the same bone in your palm, so that your hand is open and straight; then place the ligature, as if you were turning your hand at the wrist between the fingers with grain.
If a fracture occurs in one of the phalanges of the fingers, then if it is the finger tip, equalize it according to what is seen and then strengthen it by placing your palm on it; if you wish, place a small splint on the side so that the fracture is elevated and not depressed.
If the fracture is in the other fingers, as in the middle finger, or the index, or the ring finger, then equalize and bind it to the finger where that one belongs; or bind them all according to continuity, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the fingertip.
And consider, in your restoration, and nearby, the placement of cauteries and opposition—during your operation—according to what is seen when any cause from those occurs, as indicated in the narrative.
Chapter XIV.
For the restoration of fracture of the hip bone.
The hip bone, when fractured, is felt to shift according to its displacement both anteriorly and posteriorly.
And the restoration is done by first strengthening the ligaments over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture. The patient is then laid on his side and on his face.
And then the surgeon extends the ligament over its proper part according to the quality of the fracture, when it is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the hip, then extend the ligament over the part below the iliac bone, so that the extension reaches there, and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the iliac bone, then place the ligament over the iliac bone, and extend it to the parts below.
And then the doctor aligns the bone by bringing his hands close to where you have returned it according to its natural form, and the bone is joined by good articulation; then apply a poultice and bandage, so as not to cause inflammation in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then you let that be for a few days until the abscess is reduced by cauterization, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied as follows: first, wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and secure it to prevent any excess; then wrap the bandage so that it reaches the ankle and the heel of the foot.
And insert a long needle between the thigh and the nearby bandage at the fracture site, ensuring that the ends of the needle protrude significantly from both sides, and then revolve the bandage over the thigh and foot with any excess of the bandage.
And then place something on the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place a stick over the bone of the foot.
And then fill the space between the waist and the thigh with many towels, so that the contraction is constricted.
And then begin a strong contraction of the muscles over the location of the fracture with three or four layers, and the farther you are from the site of the fracture, the lesser, lighter, and more moist your contraction should be.
And then extend to both ends of the fiber into the parts where it reaches the cavity of the foot, and strengthen both ends of the threads of the other part so that the tissue does not shift from its place.
And then let the contraction remain like this so as not to injure the limb with cautery, abscess, or inflammation and resemble those; if any of these causes occur, then note it carefully, and correct what happens immediately, according to what we have said previously.
If in the bone there is a serious injury that requires placement of a splint, then make sure you equalize it as much as possible; if not, work on it and maintain it, and treat the area where the memory of the injury is present until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the pelvis is reinforced with straps or bands that are adjusted to the stellates muscles, as we have said in the assistant and in the arm.
But still in the restoration in such a manner made no claudication occurs to the patient; and if it is restored alone, or that the other leg is applied, then it is necessary that the one of which it is spoken claudicate.
And know that the pelvis is reinforced in about ten days or a few months, and all this is according to the diversity of the complications and other dispositions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the tibia.
Know that the fracture of the tibia occurs less frequently, but it often happens more than a few days with atrophy.
But if the fracture occurs, then it is not a complete break but a crack or a tear in its parts, and this is either one crack or several cracks, and about this everything depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is attempted to be equalized what is broken from the parts of it among the fingers into which it is added and joined according to what is possible by equality, facilitation, and fabrication.
And then place the plaster and hold over that round plate, if you need it; strengthen above at the stricture to what is suitable.
And then consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in other fractures, thus as for abscesses and appearance to those that arise when opposing to every accident what is suitable until it is healed.
Capitol. XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the flank.
Know that in the flank there are two bones, of which one is large and is named after the flank; the other is thin and is named fragile.
And accidents to the species of fracture occur as they do in the two bones of the arm, and for this reason, its restoration is done similarly to the restoration of the arm, and its operation is one.
And if you need to break two bones at once, the joint becomes dislocated in all directions.
And if the finer bone is broken, the joint becomes dislocated in front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find the fracture, and this is one of those causes that are beyond your control.
And then administer extension, equalization, and ligature of the tendons according to this method.

And then wrap each one in two layers of cloth according to the length of the foot, and place one layer over the back of the leg on one side and the other layer on the other side, and let them be close to the bottom base location of the foot.

Chapter XVII.
On the fracture of the talar bone and the bones of the foot and toes.
Fractures do not occur in the talus bone; more commonly, fractures affect the bones of the foot; and fractures of the toes of the foot are rare, occurring only when they are improperly handled.
And if fractures occur in the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones need to be elevated over each other, then place the patient's foot on a prepared ground as shown.
Then place the other foot on what is elevated beneath those bones, and compress, press, and align those bones back into their proper places.
Then apply a plaster and bandage over it, and place the sole of the foot on a small table with two convex sides.
Then firmly hold the foot in this position, ensuring that you have wrapped it with cloths and other materials as needed.
And when three or four days have passed, then you untie the bandage, for you will find the bones equalized, or broken, or dislocated, and the operation in those cases is as you know.
And if some are said to be broken, then restore those, and equalize them as I and have shown you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And then place the broken finger in position according to the length of the finger, and a little to the side of it; and place the skin on this table which you have previously prepared, and tighten that bandage properly.
And if what is broken of the bones are two or three or more, then you place each broken bone on its own finger slightly above it, enveloped in a piece of linen, and support the table under the base of the foot, and standing over one of the ends of the table, puncture the sole of the foot to ensure good retention.
And you should remember not to overlook that all the causes mentioned to you in the other fractures of the accidents we have discussed are not considered here; and you should adapt this to any similar case.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone and the patella and the groin of the man.

And it should be filled with pus and left for some days as we have advised, until it is healed.
When the bone of the thigh of a man or woman is broken, then administer in the restoration and needle treatment of it what we have advised for the bone of the hip.

In the case of the thigh of a man when it is broken, protect it from infection, and insert the thigh into it; then place over it an envelop of cloth, and let it be wrapped up, and let it be left for three days or nearly that time, until it is healed.
Chapter XIX.
For the restoration of bone fractures when they are dislocated.
It is convenient to treat those who sustain a dislocated fracture, especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the fibula, or similar. You should set and bleed that area frequently, provided that the conditions for bloodletting are present as we have said above.
And if the dislocation exudes pus, then keep setting the area and try to retain it by sprinkling saffron on it, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that day, and do not delay this, if an abscess does not occur.
But if an abscess does occur, then you should delay the restoration until the sixth day, once the abscess has subsided, and not approach it on the seventh or eighth day, for then you will cause worse accidents.
But if the bone is broken and prominent over the knee joint, then you must probe to reduce it and align it with your hands with ease and minimal extension.
But if the extension is not at your readiness and the alignment with your hands is difficult, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight inches and its width according to the size of the wound; for which reason it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of them according to the quantity needed in the treatment for any type of fracture.
And it should be round, and of small thickness so that it does not bend during the operation.
And it should have sharp ends, with edges at the outer ends; and be thick on the underside, but thin within.

In order that the extremity of the patient be elevated above the extremity of the bone.

But if you cannot reduce the bone completely as we have advised, then incise that part using the scalpel which we have mentioned; it may be one of the blades in any manner that is possible for you.
And then scrape out the bone of roughness and small fragments.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction, if the patient experiences severe pain in the bone, then you should know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; then do what you have been taught to give the patient great relief.
When your restoration of the mouth is completed, and then immerse a cloth in vinegar that is tightly bound with a black string, and precisely during summer, and do not remain over the wound too long or any other cause which might be oily, so that no accident such as inflammation and corruption occur.
And then administer steams at the hour of the completion of the bone restoration, and leave the wound uncovered to ensure it can breathe, using scissors where necessary and employing bandages according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not irritate the wound near the fracture, for once the doctors have made their incisions, it is easy to cause death or a severe and corrosive illness in your patients. This is for their health. And if the pain is slight and moist, different from the pain of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear certain adverse accidents that we have mentioned, and find pain in the area, then do not remain over those steams too long. Instead, use hard cloth bandages in place of steams and strengthen it with those.
And when about one or two days have passed since the sore began to form a blister, then you lift up the cloth you had placed over that area, and soon apply blisters, ointments which according to our custom we use to treat sores, thus applying tetrafarmacum and similar substances to it.
And you should remove the bandage and examine the sore at midday and in the evening while it is consolidated and closed, and ensure that the limb is prepared in such a manner that the pus drains from it with your assistance.
If the sore passes many days and is neither consolidated nor has the blister been removed from it, then you should know that here are situated the small blisters, so examine the sore by pricking it. And of those blisters, those that are separated remain and are extracted, while those that do not separate and cause pain in the limb, forcefully incise them as much as possible.

Capitol.xx.
For the cure of a swelling that occurs in the region of some fracture.
Visible accidents occur in this swelling in the region of the fracture's healing, especially near the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb; therefore, you strongly prohibit the limb from performing its natural functions.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer those medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloes, saffron, mirra, acacia, and resemble those; try to use some or all of them, confirm that they are genuine astringents, or made from white oak bark, or are genuinely good. Place one over the swelling in a cloth, and apply it tightly around it with proper constriction.
