But if the knot is already in the vein tightened and needs to be loosened, then remove that knot temporarily, and split it along the upper part of it, and incise the superfluous and prominent part, or let it rot away with a cautery during which time it should be reformed; and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter XXI.
For the restoration of fractures, when restored and then the member is subtly returned according to its natural position.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then the member is subtly returned according to its natural position, this is done for several causes: one of which is the excess of ligament solution and the joining of the ligaments not following how it should; or due to excess constriction of the ligaments during which nutrition cannot flow to the member; or due to excess ointment application; or due to excessive unnecessary movement; or due to a lack of strength in the body of the patient and its weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient light food and keep his body soft while the disease multiplies within him; administer baths; and insert tubes and joy into him.
And place your hand over the member, so that the hand draws in nutrition, or continue the application of the cold lotion, while the nutrition flows and returns to its natural form.
Capitol.xxii.
For the treatment of broken bones when they are reduced tortuously and prohibit the operation from which this results.
When the member, already reduced near its healing from tortuosity and prominence of the broken bone and nodularity, occurs in the form of a deformity of the member, but nevertheless the member is not prohibited from its natural operation, then you should not receive the word of him who thinks that member is newly broken.
Nevertheless, there are many foolish physicians and reducers who have done this in our land; this operation is greatly reproachable, leading to great fear from the sign of which is fatal.
If claudication and nodulation are recent, then soak them in a mixture of goat's milk in which you have put softening herbs such as althea, radix raziti, melilot, similar to those; and make for it a poultice with a well-made demulcent paste; or that it be soaked in mucilage of raziti and wrapped with linen cloth or oil of cedar, and applied with that; or that it be dressed with fatty figs which are macerated in pigeon's dung, similar to those mentioned in the medicines called amermans consolidatio.
And sometimes it is dissolved with light friction that is applied to the more humid areas, and movement of the limb should be administered at all times in all hours.
And if tortuosity has already gone on for some time and is strong, converted into a stone, and provoked, it is necessary to cure it by cutting. Then, the pus above the cecum and the continuation of the bone should be dissolved and incised. What is superfluous from the nodulation of that part should be removed from the bone with sharp scissors, and facilitation should be administered in that area with solicitude and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On speech concerning dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of that part, making it forbidden to move, and altering the shape of the limb, causing great pain and injuries in the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is important to quickly ensure that it is reduced, and not delay the reduction, for if delayed, the area may become gangrenous, making the reduction of the dislocation much more difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be cut or extended during the time of gangrene, for moist wounds cause spasms and intense pain.
More so when this occurs, it is advisable to perform a flarethotomy on the foot, and allow the gangrene to rest until it has subsided; then the limb should be soaked in warm water and oil, and finally, the dislocation should be reduced with ease.
And every member be treated with the water from which its memory will return to its place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the part of the body first, then proceed.
Chapter XXIV.
For the treatment of dislocation of the jaw, proceed.
When both jaws are dislocated without a fracture; and the dislocation of both is performed according to one of two methods: either they are slightly moved from their places with little movement and slightly softened; or they are completely dislocated and fully reduced, at which point they should be softened at the point of the joint where saliva from the patient flows and cannot keep that position nor close its mouth entirely, and the tongue of the patient is wrapped in a cloth.
More often than not, when the dislocation is slight, it returns to its place with little cause in major displacements.
More often than not, when the dislocation is complete and full, you must immediately administer reduction of it, and not delay it entirely.
And the minister should hold the patient's head, and the doctor insert his thumb from one hand into the side of the dislocation in the mouth on that side if the dislocation is on one side; or he should insert both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, with the other fingers holding the cheeks; and command the patient to cease moving his jaw and bring those going in all directions back; and the doctor equalize the jaw and push it back into its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are completely dislocated, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, while performing a light reduction of both, and do not delay the reduction of both entirely, as we have said before.
And when returned and equalized, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not softened, then place it over those powders of cloth with cerot, made of wax and oil rubbed together; and soon it will be facilitated with many lint pads.
And let the patient sleep on their back, with their head standing between two pillows, so that it does not sweat on either the right or left side; and do not force them to vomit for any cause; having their food consisting of a moderate amount of bread, until when the pains are relieved and the jaws are moistened, then let them sweat as they wish.
And this should be administered with mildness, and you must not trouble the appetite of the mouth; let them eat and drink as they can, until when the jaw is healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws when dislocated at any hour and cannot be returned to their proper places, then swelling and frequent fever occur, and strongly moving the abdomen of the patient causes diarrhea and pure vomiting.
And when you see this, then know it is dangerous; and strong swelling may occur to the one upon whom this happens within a few days.
Chapter 25.
For the reduction of the dislocation of the furcula and the extremity of the muscle.
The furcula is not dislocated from the joint inside by its continuation to the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer part, and this is declared by the feeling.

Chapter 26.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated in any of three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the tendon to the bone.
And the second is when it is dislocated from above the muscle, which is called taut; and it is not dislocated at the rear parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated outward and below the parts where the tendon joins the bone, and more easily in those where there are few muscles, it is slightly dislocated; more difficulty is encountered in those where there are many muscles.
And often such people are struck by a blow or fall, and the muscle of the affected area develops an abscess due to a wound, and is thus already dislocated.
Then cover what you have sprinkled there, especially over him, and proceed with the cure of him.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the joints; you must probe; so that you can distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the sound one, which you find to have a manifest difference; and you find at the head of the muscle a concavity; by probing the head of the muscle, you feel it like a hollow, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
If it is dislocated, probe the foot or above it, and then you will find a clear sensation upon touch, and it is not hidden.
And this dislocation is gently reduced when it is fresh and the patient is in pain.
And the reduction is that the minister raises the patient's hand above the joints; then place your thumbs on either side and probe the area, elevate the joint with strength at its location, and the minister raises the patient's hand, extending it properly; then place your fingers there so that it returns gently.
If, however, it does not return by what we have said, and the dislocation occurs from its initial beginning, for several days, then the patient should be bathed in warm water and a wet compress that softens and lubricates should be applied as if made of roots of almond, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and this should be administered.
And then the patient should be placed face down and kept in this position until the wool felt is equal between levity and stiffness, and then the doctor places his hand on the spine, raises the head of the muscle by its virtue, and pulls the patient's hand so that it does not get stuck at the joints, and another attendant holds the head of the patient to prevent it from getting stuck at the joints, for there it will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce the second as follows: and let there be present a man who is longer than the patient, standing on that side of the limb, and insert his muscle under the skin of the patient and raise the skin above the joint while the patient is suspended in the air, and another attendant pull the patient's hand from the joints below his abdomen. But if the patient is light, then find what other cause is suspended, so that the serious one returns quickly.
And it is reduced by another method: and it is that the patient be placed on the ground with a long stick whose head is round in shape, like the bottom of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, and then place the patient; then cover its head with lion skins, and the patient stand according to the length of the stick. And extend your hand at the parts below from the other part; and extend the body from the other part with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult due to all the causes we have said, then use this cure: and it is that you take a stick whose length is two cubits, and its width is four fingers, and its thickness be enlarged at the base according to this form.
And place lion skins over the round head so that the stick does not slip off the patient.
And then place the patient's legs outstretched, and extend your hand or arm along the side of the body between the parts, and tie the limb to the supporter and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
And then place the arm on a step of the ladder according to its width, and the hand extended between the parts, and the other side be suspended from the other part, that the joint may soon come together.
And when the instrument is complete according to any manner that is possible for you, then take a waiting thread of wool of equal quantity; and place a plaster prepared of powder of moly ammen and honey over all the muscle, thus thoroughly mixed on top, and strongly tie the thread around the area; let the plaster be thoroughly applied over the thread, and the hand be attached to the collar of the patient, and do not move the hand for seven days.
And ensure that the diet of the patient is light, until the limb is comforted, for it is easier to recover.
And then the food should be given more seven or five times and exercise should be performed; and thus if it is closed and not softened, then it is already healed.
And if the joint is dislocated due to repeated humidity that affects it or for another cause, then you must apply a cautery three times, as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the ligament and joint are not closed, and the plaster and stricture remain over it for several times, and still are not closed with catgut and the arm cannot be raised above the parts above, then you should know that the nerves of it, which are at the head of the muscle, are either incised or extended or softened, and you should know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.

And it should be applied to the other stricture, and care should be taken that it be healed within.

Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the joint of the shoulder is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; it can be dislocated in all directions, but most commonly in front and behind. The dislocation is not hidden; whether you see it quickly or feel it by touch depending on the figure that is dislocated, when you reduce the dislocated shoulder joint into place, this is clearly manifest to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm or reach its own muscle.
And note that when it comes to the collarbone, reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before an abscess forms; for if an abscess forms, reduction is difficult, and even with a strong effort, it may not be fully healed. Especially behind, for this is worse than all other types of dislocation, causing more pain, and warm poultices can lead to its death.

And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong extension and a firm movement.
And let two ministers extend the arm, and hold that position with two ministers so that the extension is not reversed.
And then rotate the arm completely around, as it is wrapped in a long folded cloth around the patient's hand, or bandage.

And if you see the joint not well strengthened, then reapply the poultice and ligament, and leave it for some days until it is healed; and then release it.

Capitol. xxviii.
For the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder, it is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.

But more importantly, one should consider if the palm is already placed on the abdomen of the hand, then the patient places the two hands on a table to extend and reduce; and if the dislocation has preceded, then place the two hands, while intrinsically positioning the hand of the physician over the table, so that the physician's hand falls over that potentiality of the joint.
And so if it returns quickly, that is good; and otherwise, then strengthen that by binding the abscess and leave it as it is; and do not repeat this, for if you do not, you should neither endure nor be able to force anything beyond what passes in his days.
But more importantly, the joint is to be treated according to its tortuosity; and do not notify the patient any cause, but let your hand be softened and cannot strengthen any cause; then you know that the nerves are taut or stretched, and then there is no need in it but to strengthen it by cautery near the insertion and make it firm with this you confer some cause.
And since the joint has returned, then place it over the binding which we have recommended, and let it be softened and strengthened for five days; and then release it and let it be left, and exercise the hand of the patient.
But if the movement in it has been sustained and occurred with some gravity, then gently apply that which has warm water and some friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Capitol.xxix.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
In some cases, dislocate the fingers all at once.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated at the base of the palm or in the finger itself, then hold the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs, until it returns to place.
And then hold the tip of the finger, and suspend that part which is dislocated, leaving it for two days.
And then suspend that part and extend it until it is straightened in that same day.
And when it is night, tie it according to that narration, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise with movement, and tie it at night; do this for several days until it straightens.
And make it seem as if you are doing so, even if dislocated in the hand, and bind that to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And make it seem as if you are doing so when it is dislocated in the other parts.
Capitol.
For the cure of dislocation of the cervical vertebrae.
When a dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or the neck, and multiple vertebrae are displaced from their places, then there is no cure for that, for death will follow.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excretion is forced out against their will, and they cannot retain it; often several times, the limbs, or both sides, or one side of the body become soft. Or a vertebra is displaced from its place, for multiple times a vertebra is displaced, and multiple times the removal of those to four parts is performed.
Then it is already reduced to the parts behind and is named gelbozitation.
May the cure of it be that when you consider if the gelbozitation occurs from pregnancy, then in that case there is no cure nor will it heal completely.
May it be that in those cases that arise from birth or by percussion or similar to those, the Ancients have already detailed the methods of cure at great length, about which the best is reserved for prophecy; but I shall now abbreviate what I know of that, for the sake of simplicity and proximity of my explanation, with which I have expanded and arranged it in various instruments from which I will show and expand upon that.
And of those, only the one that occurs in the two places properly so-called is cured as I recommend.
And it is that the patient be extended on his back close to the wall, and be covered with a moist cloth to prevent his feet from getting cold.
And place a straight stick, placed in the ground at the end of the back near its head, and another stick across its feet at the other end of the stick.
The minister hold the stick, and it should not be fixed in the ground; another minister hold the other end according to that form.
And wrap a cloth around the patient's head and tie it tightly with a leather thong; extend the end of the thong to the stick which is placed at the head, and fasten that end on it. And wrap another leather thong around the patient's hips under his armpits, and support his legs; and adjust all the thongs and fasten those on the other stick which supports his feet.
And the minister carefully extend the thong to the part of the stick, and do not move the two sticks and the thongs in which they are placed, but do not fix them as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or he places one hand on a table and adjusts his feet on the table as it returns.
And if it does not return in this manner, then take a table of which the width or length is about three braces, and cut into the wall which we said is close to the patient a place between the ends of the table. And place the majority of the table's surface on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and presses firmly so that the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open a turn which is revolved in your hand; and when you stand on the ground, place the patient's head at the heel of two sticks, the length of which is one brace, and place his head at the heel of two sticks similarly. The distance between each stick should be one palm. Now draw a circle around each stick with thread, and make sure each is firmly fixed to the ground so that it cannot move.
And there should be a round stake on which the torn part is placed, and it should be turned in the two cuts of the two stakes.
And at the end of it is a cut on which the stake is formed, the length of which is one palm's breadth as it is turned; and in the other two stakes it is similar to that.
And then strengthen the ligaments you have stretched in the feet of the patient in the cut where you place its head, and the ligaments you have stretched in the thighs of the patient in the wound where you place its feet.
And then you should place each wound and have a minister twist the wound with his hand, and the doctor straighten the spine as we have said.
And once the spine is returned and the area is straightened, then cover it with a paste poultice of swine's grease, and some wool; place a strip of cloth over the paste, the width of which should be about three fingers or nearly those, and its length should accommodate the area of the yellow bile, and over the part of the spine.
And then apply the incense which we have mentioned, and use the patient of subtle nourishment under which it heals.
And if there is any danger at the place near the end of the healing, then it should be treated with medicines that moderate and soothe over a long period as we have recommended; and already a lead plate has been administered in that place.
Sometimes dangers occur near the end of the vertebrae in the back, and are caused by that dislocation; it is that bone where you already suspect that no strong medicine should be applied to it, as it might cause your patient to die.
Capitol.xxxxi.
On the treatment of dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle should occur only from dislocation, and not from what occurs in other joints due to slight movement and凹陷。
And the joint of the wrist is dislocated according to four manners, which is dislocated to the inner parts, or to the outer parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the inner parts, and rarely to the front or back parts.
And the sign of dislocation to the inner parts is that when you adjust the leg of the healthy side against the affected side, the injured part becomes longer and the swelling is more prominent than on the healthy side. The affected limb cannot bend its foot, so it must be stretched. This is the place where one should apply a cauterization to an abscess, so that the head of the swelling has been reached here.
And the sign of dislocation to the outer parts is that the symptoms of the injury are contrary to this dislocation.
And the sign of dislocation to the front parts is that the swelling expands according to its complement, but it cannot bend the part where the pain is located. If forced to move towards the front parts, it cannot do so; and his urine is retained, and his congestion is an abscess. You should apply treatment to this area on the thigh.
And a sign of dislocation in the rear parts is that the buttock does not expand, nor can it bend what is before to bend the injured part, and the thigh is shorter than the other, and the injured part is softened, and the groin is displaced to that location.
The main reduction of these species of dislocation is that you consider, at the time, if the dislocation is old, whether it has already hardened. If so, then after you have had it near you, you administer the reduction according to its disposition, but not in all of that treatment; then one should not conjecture about it.
For the one where the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, then you should attend to this by reducing the joint and extending it to the internal and external parts, and move it to the right and left, as it will naturally return and you will not need any other treatment.
Moreover, if it does not return in this manner, then you should prepare a strong remedy and extend the thigh from the part left out, or on its hands, or with linen which is tied over the thigh above the buttock; another remedy extends the hand from above so that it touches the tips of its feet; and apply hot linseed over the groin, and have another person hold the end of the linen.
And it is an extension of the lung or from the inside part to the angle at the forked part, or from the back part to the side of the diaphragm.
And it is the extension of all those in one swoop where the patient's body rises from the ground and bends.
And this species of extension is special for all four.
And so, if the dislocation returns as we have said, it will be good; otherwise, for every species, what I say about its own cure is necessary.
In the reduction of its own, when dislocated, and the dislocation is from the inside parts, make sure the patient lies on their side; then place the bandages around the thigh in that location where between the base of the thigh and where the bandage meets the angle of the hip. And extend the bandage from the part of the hip to the other side of the forked part.
And then another minister firmly holds the thighs, compresses the thick part of the thigh, and extends to the outer parts by strong extension, so that it returns to its place.
And this species is of such gentle cure for which this member is reduced more than other species.
And if it is excused for you and you do not obey the instruction for this cure, then ensure that both feet of the patient are tied together firmly but gently over both ankles and insteps.
And let the length of each be that of four fingers, and let the patient's leg be extended more than the other by the amount of two fingers.
And then hang the patient's leg over the top of the stake, and let it be supported by the hand; and let the length of the rope be that of two braces.
And command a strong young man to hold the patient's thigh, and let another strong man hang the leg, with one holding the thigh firmly to help return the joint to its place quickly.
And in the proper reduction, when dislocation occurs at the outer part, ensure that the patient lies on his side according to what we have recommended for lateral decubitus; and strengthen the bandage around the affected leg and over its ankle.
And then two poles be placed, one on the foot and the other on the head; and then poles adjusted be placed in the middle of the stake fixed over which wet cloths be wrapped, so that the patient is not damaged by the pole being between their thighs, for you must allow the extension of the parts left undone.
And then each minister extend to their part, and the doctor with his hand equalize the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is good that the leg be slightly extended, and this according to this narrative method for the stake; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the gouty joint, and then ties it with the other hand, and with that tie he fixes the extension at the back part relative to the front part.
If the dislocation is at the rear parts, then do not allow the patient to stretch out his legs and stand on the ground; instead, elevate him above the ground. You should place him on a hard surface, just as you would if the width of the joint were dislocated outward, as we said regarding its position between two widths; and his face and thighs should be stretched out, as we have said, with little difficulty.
A compress should be applied on the area where the joint protrudes.
So, when the reduction is completed as it should — and you notice the sign of the reduction, which is that you can extend both legs of the patient, and when you test them and find that the limb returns to its normal position — then adjust the thighs and apply padding and wrap tightly so that the width is not altered in any part; and adhere the limb to rest for three or four days.
Then remove the bandage and padding, and compare one leg with the other.
When you see the equal marks in the wound, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of swelling, then return and apply a plaster there, and extend that according to your first structure, and let it be for three days.
Then loosen that, and let it have an ambulation on each part for some days until it is comfortable.
Capitol.xxxii.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder is dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated to the outward parts, to the inward parts, and to the front parts more than to the back parts; it is not dislocated to the rear parts.
And the sign of shoulder dislocation is that when you command the patient to apply their hand to their hip; then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And the restoration of all these ways is that you make sure the patient lies straight, try to extend his side as much as possible; attach your two hands to the face of him, and place his foot between your feet; then make sure the sole of him adheres to your palms, hold those between your toes on the sole of him with strength, and another minister extends his foot so that the sole returns to its place.
And the sign of relief is that his side adheres to the flank with light, effortless ease.
And then apply that and make the side adhere to the flank, and place those continuously with a bandage for three or four days, and later remove those.
And do not use the patient for labor for some days until he is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it is excused to you, then administer strong extension to the limbs of which in the treatment of the hip the previous narration was given.
Chapter XXXIII.
For the treatment of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes reduced by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated according to the injury, and the dislocation of the ankle is performed either on the inner parts or on the outer parts.
And the sign of the ankle dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen coming from the part to which it is dislocated.
More the treatment of reducing the ankle is by gentle movement, and it is that the swelling be reduced with ease and return to its normal position.
More in the treatment of the ankle when it is dislocated according to the injury, make sure you correct the deformity properly, and have a strong and brave assistant behind the injured limb at the midpoint of the leg.
And then place your right hand on the top part of the foot, and with your left hand hold the lower part of the foot at the location of the heel.
And then draw the foot to your right hand, and towards the left by two passes, and extend the left; and push the foot on the right should lower the leg well.
And do this twice as we have counted.
And then push the foot towards the thigh for the third pass, and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two passes in this manner, and if you grasp the foot properly, that is good; otherwise, then iterate the operation, for it may not have returned.
But if the reduction is forbidden to you as we have prescribed, then place the patient on his side on the ground, and insert a firm stick between the patient's thighs, and you have already wrapped it with a cloth to prevent the stick from touching the patient.
And then the minister hold their thighs, and another minister extends the foot with their hands or with a cloth tied around the ankle of the foot; and then each minister extends themselves against the extension of their part, and the stick remains between the patient's thighs holding them, so that the body of the patient is not drawn to the sides as you lower its extension.
And then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands, while another assistant holds the healthy thigh at the points left, so that the dislocation returns with ease.
And then as the dislocation returns and its reduction is declared to you as healthy, then apply a plaster and bind the foot with strong lint to the leg.
And beware of the nerves behind the ankle, for strong lint over that area might irritate and severely affect them.
And then leave that area for two or three days; and if the lint has become moist, change it.
And then remove the lint on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for forty days. But if it is necessary for the patient to walk in that area, then there is no guarantee that the dislocation is not dissolved and corrupted, and such cases do not respond to treatment.
And if an abscess occurs at that site, then ensure that you administer as we described in the previous part of our narrative, in another location for treatment and fomentation, until it is ripe.
Chapter 34.
For the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
First, ensure that what is dislocated can be reduced with little extension; and whatever is not difficult, it is easier to do so quickly.
But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the foot, then you should place the patient's foot on a flat surface or on a table, and have them lie as if they were sleeping.
Then stand on the patient's foot, and place your hand over what you suspect to be the joint. Gently push that area with your hand to promote its return to place, and observe whether it has been reduced and no prominence appears in the area.
Then place the patient's foot on a table that can hold the entire foot, which has two ends. Gently maintain this position with a strong, steady structure for three days; then release it and allow the foot to rest by walking for several days, until it is strengthened and reliability is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that occur from blows or fractures or any other way.
When any of these causes occur, and you are involved in the treatment and restoration thereof, mishaps may lead to death.
And for this reason, you should not presume in the treatment of it, but rather someone who is skilled in the art, long accustomed to its use, merciful and pious; one who waits, not eager or presumptuous. He should use medicines at the beginning that allow the abscesses to come to a head with cautery only, leaving the fate to divine judgment, but to whom you hope for a cure, for alleviation of the illness, in which some hope appears. And then strive to perform the reduction at the beginning of the cause, before it develops into an abscess.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, proceed to administer treatment that allows the abscess to come to a head with cautery and heals the wound according to what is suitable for it using drying ointments.
And if dislocation or fracture occurs in the separated bones of the foot, then you must realign their joint, and do so as we have said in the simple ailments that precede here in those places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the instrument in the ways of fear according to which my preaching precedes you; for this is better for you from your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more greetings to your true essence those in your time. Thus you finish the words of Al-Bucasis perfectly, for which may God bless him. Amen.
